   <h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">
    Callisto (moon)
   </h1>
   <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
    <div class="noprint" id="siteSub">
     From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia
    </div>
    <div id="contentSub">
    </div>
    <div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
     Jump to:
     <a href="#mw-head">
      navigation
     </a>
     ,
     <a href="#p-search">
      search
     </a>
    </div>
    <div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en">
     <div class="mw-parser-output">
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       For other uses, see
       <a class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Callisto_(disambiguation)" title="Callisto (disambiguation)">
        Callisto
       </a>
       .
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Not to be confused with
       <a href="/wiki/Calypso_(moon)" title="Calypso (moon)">
        Calypso (moon)
       </a>
       .
      </div>
      <table class="infobox" style="width:22em">
       <caption class="fn org">
        Callisto
       </caption>
       <tr>
        <td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">
         <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Callisto.jpg">
          <img alt="Callisto.jpg" data-file-height="753" data-file-width="740" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Callisto.jpg/221px-Callisto.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Callisto.jpg/332px-Callisto.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Callisto.jpg/442px-Callisto.jpg 2x" width="221"/>
         </a>
         <div>
          Callisto's anti-Jovian hemisphere imaged in 2001 by NASA's
          <i>
           <a href="/wiki/Galileo_(spacecraft)" title="Galileo (spacecraft)">
            Galileo
           </a>
          </i>
          spacecraft. It shows a heavily cratered terrain. The large
          <a href="/wiki/Complex_crater" title="Complex crater">
           impact structure
          </a>
          <a href="/wiki/Asgard_(crater)" title="Asgard (crater)">
           Asgard
          </a>
          is on the limb at upper right. The prominent
          <a href="/wiki/Ray_system" title="Ray system">
           rayed crater
          </a>
          below and just right of center is
          <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Bran_(crater)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Bran (crater) (page does not exist)">
           Bran
          </a>
          .
         </div>
        </td>
       </tr>

        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th scope="row" style="max-width:11em;">
         <div style="padding:0.1em 0;line-height:1.2em;">

          </a>
         </div>
        </th>
        <td>
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Synchronous_rotation" title="Synchronous rotation">
          synchronous
         </a>
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Anderson_2001_5-3">
          <a href="#cite_note-Anderson_2001-5">
           [3]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>

          </a>
         </div>
        </th>
        <td>
         zero
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Anderson_2001_5-4">
          <a href="#cite_note-Anderson_2001-5">
           [3]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th scope="row" style="max-width:11em;">

         </a>
        </th>

         0.22 (geometric)
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moore2004_11-0">
          <a href="#cite_note-Moore2004-11">

          </a>
         </sup>
        </td>
       </tr>

         <span class="nowrap">

         up to
         <span class="nowrap">

          </sup>
         </span>

         </a>
         (O
         <sub>
          2
         </sub>

         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Liang_2005_14-0">

       <tr>
        <td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">
         <div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">
         </div>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       <b>
        Callisto
       </b>
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt">
         <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">
          /
          <span style="border-bottom:1px dotted">
           <span title="'k' in 'kind'">
            k
           </span>
           <span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">
            ə
           </span>
           <span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">
            ˈ
           </span>
           <span title="'l' in 'lie'">
            l
           </span>
           <span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">
            ɪ
           </span>
           <span title="'s' in 'sigh'">
            s
           </span>
           <span title="'t' in 'tie'">
            t
           </span>
           <span title="/oʊ/: 'o' in 'code'">
            oʊ
           </span>
          </span>
          /
         </a>
        </span>
       </span>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
        <a href="#cite_note-15">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       (
       <b>
        Jupiter IV
       </b>
       ) is the second-largest
       <a href="/wiki/Moons_of_Jupiter" title="Moons of Jupiter">
        moon of Jupiter
       </a>
       , after
       <a href="/wiki/Ganymede_(moon)" title="Ganymede (moon)">
        Ganymede
       </a>
       . It is the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_moons#Moons_of_planets_and_dwarf_planets" title="List of moons">
        third-largest moon
       </a>
       in the
       <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">
        Solar System
       </a>
       after Ganymede and
       <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">
        Saturn
       </a>
       's largest moon
       <a href="/wiki/Titan_(moon)" title="Titan (moon)">
        Titan
       </a>
       , and the largest object in the Solar System not to be properly
       <a href="/wiki/Planetary_differentiation" title="Planetary differentiation">
        differentiated
       </a>
       . Callisto was discovered in 1610 by
       <a href="/wiki/Galileo_Galilei" title="Galileo Galilei">
        Galileo Galilei
       </a>
       . At
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
         7006482100000000000♠
        </span>
        4821 km
       </span>
       in diameter, Callisto has about 99% the diameter of the planet
       <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">
        Mercury
       </a>
       but only about a third of its mass. It is the fourth
       <a href="/wiki/Galilean_moons" title="Galilean moons">
        Galilean moon
       </a>
       of
       <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">
        Jupiter
       </a>
       by distance, with an orbital radius of about
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
         7009188300000000000♠
        </span>
        1
        <span style="margin-left:.25em;">
         883
        </span>
        <span style="margin-left:.25em;">
         000
        </span>
        km
       </span>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-orbit_4-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-orbit-4">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It is not in an
       <a href="/wiki/Orbital_resonance" title="Orbital resonance">
        orbital resonance
       </a>
       like the three other Galilean satellites—
       <a href="/wiki/Io_(moon)" title="Io (moon)">
        Io
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Europa_(moon)" title="Europa (moon)">
        Europa
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Ganymede_(moon)" title="Ganymede (moon)">
        Ganymede
       </a>
       —and is thus not appreciably
       <a href="/wiki/Tidal_heating" title="Tidal heating">
        tidally heated
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Musotto2002_16-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Musotto2002-16">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Callisto's rotation is
       <a href="/wiki/Tidal_locking" title="Tidal locking">
        tidally locked
       </a>
       to its orbit around Jupiter, so that the same hemisphere always faces inward; Jupiter appears to stand nearly still in Callisto's sky. It is less affected by Jupiter's
       <a href="/wiki/Magnetosphere" title="Magnetosphere">
        magnetosphere
       </a>
       than the other
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inner_satellite" title="Inner satellite">
        inner satellites
       </a>
       because of its more remote orbit, located just outside Jupiter's main radiation belt.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cooper2001_17-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Cooper2001-17">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
        <a href="#cite_note-18">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Callisto is composed of approximately equal amounts of
       <a href="/wiki/Rock_(geology)" title="Rock (geology)">
        rock
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Volatiles" title="Volatiles">
        ices
       </a>
       , with a
       <a href="/wiki/Density" title="Density">
        density
       </a>
       of about
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
         7003183000000000000♠
        </span>
        1.83 g/cm
        <sup>
         3
        </sup>
       </span>
       , the lowest density and surface gravity of Jupiter's major moons. Compounds detected
       <a href="/wiki/Spectroscopy" title="Spectroscopy">
        spectroscopically
       </a>
       on the surface include
       <a href="/wiki/Ice" title="Ice">
        water ice
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20150315_19-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-NYT-20150315-19">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">
        carbon dioxide
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Silicate" title="Silicate">
        silicates
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Organic_compound" title="Organic compound">
        organic compounds
       </a>
       . Investigation by the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Galileo_(spacecraft)" title="Galileo (spacecraft)">
         Galileo
        </a>
       </i>
       spacecraft revealed that Callisto may have a small
       <a href="/wiki/Silicate" title="Silicate">
        silicate
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Planetary_core" title="Planetary core">
        core
       </a>
       and possibly a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Subsurface_ocean" title="Subsurface ocean">
        subsurface ocean
       </a>
       of liquid
       <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">
        water
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20150315_19-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-NYT-20150315-19">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       at depths greater than
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">
         7005100000000000000♠
        </span>
        100 km
       </span>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kuskov2005_20-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Kuskov2005-20">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Showman1999_9-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Showman1999-9">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The surface of Callisto is the oldest and most heavily
       <a href="/wiki/Impact_crater" title="Impact crater">
        cratered
       </a>
       in the Solar System.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
        <a href="#cite_note-21">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Its surface is completely covered with impact craters.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
        <a href="#cite_note-22">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It does not show any signatures of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endogenic" title="Endogenic">
        subsurface
       </a>
       processes such as
       <a href="/wiki/Plate_tectonics" title="Plate tectonics">
        plate tectonics
       </a>
       or
       <a href="/wiki/Volcano" title="Volcano">
        volcanism
       </a>
       , with no signs that geological activity in general has ever occurred, and is thought to have evolved predominantly under the influence of
       <a href="/wiki/Impact_crater" title="Impact crater">
        impacts
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greeley_2000_23-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Greeley_2000-23">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Prominent surface features include multi-ring structures, variously shaped
       <a href="/wiki/Impact_crater" title="Impact crater">
        impact craters
       </a>
       , and chains of craters (
       <i>
        catenae
       </i>
       ) and associated
       <a href="/wiki/Escarpment" title="Escarpment">
        scarps
       </a>
       , ridges and deposits.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greeley_2000_23-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Greeley_2000-23">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       At a small scale, the surface is varied and made up of small, sparkly frost
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deposit_(geology)" title="Deposit (geology)">
        deposits
       </a>
       at the tips of high spots, surrounded by a low-lying, smooth blanket of dark material.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moore2004_11-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Moore2004-11">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This is thought to result from the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sublimation_(chemistry)" title="Sublimation (chemistry)">
        sublimation
       </a>
       -driven degradation of small
       <a href="/wiki/Landform" title="Landform">
        landforms
       </a>
       , which is supported by the general deficit of small impact craters and the presence of numerous small knobs, considered to be their remnants.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moore1999_24-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Moore1999-24">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The absolute ages of the landforms are not known.
      </p>
      <p>
       Callisto is surrounded by an extremely thin
       <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">
        atmosphere
       </a>
       composed of
       <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">
        carbon dioxide
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carlson_1999_13-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Carlson_1999-13">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and probably
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Molecular_oxygen" title="Molecular oxygen">
        molecular oxygen
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Liang_2005_14-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Liang_2005-14">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       as well as by a rather intense
       <a href="/wiki/Ionosphere" title="Ionosphere">
        ionosphere
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kliore_2002_25-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Kliore_2002-25">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Callisto is thought to have formed by slow
       <a href="/wiki/Accretion_(astrophysics)" title="Accretion (astrophysics)">
        accretion
       </a>
       from the disk of the gas and dust that surrounded Jupiter after its formation.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Canup2002_26-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Canup2002-26">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Callisto's gradual accretion and the lack of tidal heating meant that not enough heat was available for rapid
       <a href="/wiki/Planetary_differentiation" title="Planetary differentiation">
        differentiation
       </a>
       . The slow
       <a href="/wiki/Convection" title="Convection">
        convection
       </a>
       in the interior of Callisto, which commenced soon after formation, led to partial differentiation and possibly to the formation of a subsurface ocean at a depth of 100–150 km and a small, rocky
       <a href="/wiki/Planetary_core" title="Planetary core">
        core
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spohn_2003_27-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Spohn_2003-27">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The likely presence of an ocean within Callisto leaves open the possibility that it could harbor
       <a href="/wiki/Extraterrestrial_life" title="Extraterrestrial life">
        life
       </a>
       . However, conditions are thought to be less favorable than on nearby
       <a href="/wiki/Europa_(moon)" title="Europa (moon)">
        Europa
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lipps2004_28-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Lipps2004-28">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Various space probes from
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Pioneer_10" title="Pioneer 10">
         Pioneers 10
        </a>
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Pioneer_11" title="Pioneer 11">
         11
        </a>
       </i>
       to
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Galileo_(spacecraft)" title="Galileo (spacecraft)">
         Galileo
        </a>
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Cassini%E2%80%93Huygens" title="Cassini–Huygens">
         Cassini
        </a>
       </i>
       have studied Callisto. Because of its low
       <a href="/wiki/Radiation" title="Radiation">
        radiation
       </a>
       levels, Callisto has long been considered the most suitable place for a human base for future exploration of the Jovian system.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HOPE_29-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-HOPE-29">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>

          </span>

        </li>
        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-5">

          <span class="tocnumber">

          <span class="toctext">

          </span>
         </a>
         <ul>
          <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-6">
           <a href="#Composition">
            <span class="tocnumber">

            </span>
            <span class="toctext">
             Composition
            </span>
           </a>
          </li>
          <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-7">
           <a href="#Internal_structure">
            <span class="tocnumber">
             3.2
            </span>
            <span class="toctext">
             Internal structure
            </span>
           </a>
          </li>
          <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-8">

      <p>

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="History">
        History
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Callisto_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Discovery">
        Discovery
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Callisto_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Discovery">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Callisto was discovered by Galileo in January 1610, along with the three other large Jovian moons—
       <a href="/wiki/Ganymede_(moon)" title="Ganymede (moon)">
        Ganymede
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Io_(moon)" title="Io (moon)">
        Io
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Europa_(moon)" title="Europa (moon)">
        Europa
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Galilei_1-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Galilei-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Name">
        Name
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Callisto_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Name">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Callisto is named after one of
       <a href="/wiki/Zeus" title="Zeus">
        Zeus
       </a>
       's many lovers in
       <a href="/wiki/Greek_mythology" title="Greek mythology">
        Greek mythology
       </a>
       .
       <a href="/wiki/Callisto_(mythology)" title="Callisto (mythology)">
        Callisto
       </a>
       was a nymph (or, according to some sources, the daughter of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lycaon_(mythology)" title="Lycaon (mythology)">
        Lycaon
       </a>
       ) who was associated with the goddess of the hunt,
       <a href="/wiki/Artemis" title="Artemis">
        Artemis
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Galileo_30-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Galileo-30">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The name was suggested by
       <a href="/wiki/Simon_Marius" title="Simon Marius">
        Simon Marius
       </a>
       soon after Callisto's discovery.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marius_31-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Marius-31">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Marius attributed the suggestion to
       <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Kepler" title="Johannes Kepler">
        Johannes Kepler
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Galileo_30-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Galileo-30">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, the names of the
       <a href="/wiki/Galilean_moons" title="Galilean moons">
        Galilean satellites
       </a>
       fell into disfavor for a considerable time, and were not revived in common use until the mid-20th century. In much of the earlier astronomical literature, Callisto is referred to by its Roman numeral designation, a system introduced by Galileo, as
       <b>
        <span class="nowrap">
         Jupiter IV
        </span>
       </b>
       or as "the fourth satellite of Jupiter".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barnard1892_32-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Barnard1892-32">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In scientific writing, the adjectival form of the name is
       <i>
        Callistoan
       </i>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Klemaszewski2001_33-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Klemaszewski2001-33">
         [27]
        </a>
       </sup>
       pronounced
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt">
         <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">
          /
          <span style="border-bottom:1px dotted">
           <span title="/ˌ/: secondary stress follows">
            ˌ
           </span>
           <span title="'k' in 'kind'">
            k
           </span>
           <span title="/æ/: 'a' in 'bad'">
            æ
           </span>
           <span title="'l' in 'lie'">
            l
           </span>
           <span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">
            ɪ
           </span>
           <span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">
            ˈ
           </span>
           <span title="'s' in 'sigh'">
            s
           </span>
           <span title="'t' in 'tie'">
            t
           </span>
           <span title="/oʊ/: 'o' in 'code'">
            oʊ
           </span>
           <span title="/./: syllable break">
            .
           </span>
           <span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">
            ə
           </span>
           <span title="'n' in 'nigh'">
            n
           </span>
          </span>
          /
         </a>
        </span>
       </span>
       , or
       <i>
        Callistan
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moore1999_24-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Moore1999-24">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Orbit_and_rotation">
        Orbit and rotation
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Callisto_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Orbit and rotation">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:001221_Cassini_Jupiter_%26_Europa_%26_Callisto.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1016" data-file-width="846" height="204" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/001221_Cassini_Jupiter_%26_Europa_%26_Callisto.jpg/170px-001221_Cassini_Jupiter_%26_Europa_%26_Callisto.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/001221_Cassini_Jupiter_%26_Europa_%26_Callisto.jpg/255px-001221_Cassini_Jupiter_%26_Europa_%26_Callisto.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/001221_Cassini_Jupiter_%26_Europa_%26_Callisto.jpg/340px-001221_Cassini_Jupiter_%26_Europa_%26_Callisto.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:001221_Cassini_Jupiter_%26_Europa_%26_Callisto.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Callisto (bottom left), Jupiter (top right) and Europa (below and left of Jupiter's
         <a href="/wiki/Great_Red_Spot" title="Great Red Spot">
          Great Red Spot
         </a>
         ) as viewed by
         <i>
          <a href="/wiki/Cassini%E2%80%93Huygens" title="Cassini–Huygens">
           Cassini–Huygens
          </a>
         </i>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Callisto is the outermost of the four Galilean moons of Jupiter. It orbits at a distance of approximately 1 880 000 km (26.3 times the 71 492 km radius of Jupiter itself).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-orbit_4-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-orbit-4">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This is significantly larger than the orbital radius—1 070 000 km—of the next-closest Galilean satellite, Ganymede. As a result of this relatively distant orbit, Callisto does not participate in the
       <a href="/wiki/Orbital_resonance" title="Orbital resonance">
        mean-motion resonance
       </a>
       —in which the three inner Galilean satellites are locked—and probably never has.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Musotto2002_16-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Musotto2002-16">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Like most other regular planetary moons, Callisto's rotation is locked to be
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Synchronous_rotation" title="Synchronous rotation">
        synchronous
       </a>
       with its orbit.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Anderson_2001_5-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-Anderson_2001-5">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The length of Callisto's day, simultaneously its
       <a href="/wiki/Orbital_period" title="Orbital period">
        orbital period
       </a>
       , is about 16.7 Earth days. Its orbit is very slightly eccentric and inclined to the Jovian
       <a href="/wiki/Equator" title="Equator">
        equator
       </a>
       , with the
       <a href="/wiki/Orbital_eccentricity" title="Orbital eccentricity">
        eccentricity
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inclination" title="Inclination">
        inclination
       </a>
       changing
       <a href="/wiki/Almost_periodic_function" title="Almost periodic function">
        quasi-periodically
       </a>
       due to solar and planetary gravitational perturbations on a timescale of centuries. The ranges of change are 0.0072–0.0076 and 0.20–0.60°, respectively.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Musotto2002_16-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Musotto2002-16">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       These orbital variations cause the
       <a href="/wiki/Axial_tilt" title="Axial tilt">
        axial tilt
       </a>
       (the angle between rotational and orbital axes) to vary between 0.4 and 1.6°.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bills2005_34-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Bills2005-34">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The dynamical isolation of Callisto means that it has never been appreciably
       <a href="/wiki/Tidal_heating" title="Tidal heating">
        tidally heated
       </a>
       , which has important consequences for its internal structure and
       <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">
        evolution
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Freeman2006_35-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Freeman2006-35">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Its distance from Jupiter also means that the
       <a href="/wiki/Charged_particle" title="Charged particle">
        charged-particle
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Flux" title="Flux">
        flux
       </a>
       from Jupiter's
       <a href="/wiki/Magnetosphere" title="Magnetosphere">
        magnetosphere
       </a>
       at its surface is relatively low—about 300 times lower than, for example, that at
       <a href="/wiki/Europa_(moon)" title="Europa (moon)">
        Europa
       </a>
       . Hence, unlike the other Galilean moons, charged-particle
       <a href="/wiki/Irradiation" title="Irradiation">
        irradiation
       </a>
       has had a relatively minor effect on Callisto's surface.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cooper2001_17-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Cooper2001-17">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The radiation level at Callisto's surface is equivalent to a dose of about 0.01
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/R%C3%B6ntgen_equivalent_man" title="Röntgen equivalent man">
        rem
       </a>
       (0.1
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Millisievert" title="Millisievert">
        mSv
       </a>
       ) per day, which is over ten times higher than Earth's average background radiation.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
        <a href="#cite_note-36">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ringwald_37-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ringwald-37">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_characteristics">
        Physical characteristics
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Callisto_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Physical characteristics">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Composition">
        Composition
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Callisto_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Composition">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Callisto,_Earth_%26_Moon_size_comparison.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2026" data-file-width="3000" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Callisto%2C_Earth_%26_Moon_size_comparison.jpg/220px-Callisto%2C_Earth_%26_Moon_size_comparison.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Callisto%2C_Earth_%26_Moon_size_comparison.jpg/330px-Callisto%2C_Earth_%26_Moon_size_comparison.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Callisto%2C_Earth_%26_Moon_size_comparison.jpg/440px-Callisto%2C_Earth_%26_Moon_size_comparison.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Callisto,_Earth_%26_Moon_size_comparison.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Size comparison of
         <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">
          Earth
         </a>
         ,
         <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">
          Moon
         </a>
         and Callisto
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA00844_NIMS_spectra.gif">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2036" data-file-width="2528" height="177" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/PIA00844_NIMS_spectra.gif/220px-PIA00844_NIMS_spectra.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/PIA00844_NIMS_spectra.gif/330px-PIA00844_NIMS_spectra.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/PIA00844_NIMS_spectra.gif/440px-PIA00844_NIMS_spectra.gif 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA00844_NIMS_spectra.gif" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Galileo_(spacecraft)#Near-Infrared_Mapping_Spectrometer_(NIMS)" title="Galileo (spacecraft)">
          Near-IR spectra
         </a>
         of dark cratered plains (red) and the
         <a href="/wiki/Asgard_(crater)" title="Asgard (crater)">
          Asgard impact structure
         </a>
         (blue), showing the presence of more water ice (
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Water_absorption" title="Water absorption">
          absorption bands
         </a>
         from 1 to 2
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Micrometer_(unit)" title="Micrometer (unit)">
          µm
         </a>
         )
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clark_38-0">
          <a href="#cite_note-Clark-38">
           [32]
          </a>
         </sup>
         and less rocky material within Asgard.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The average
       <a href="/wiki/Density" title="Density">
        density
       </a>
       of Callisto, 1.83 g/cm
       <sup>
        3
       </sup>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Anderson_2001_5-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-Anderson_2001-5">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       suggests a composition of approximately equal parts of rocky material and
       <a href="/wiki/Ice" title="Ice">
        water ice
       </a>
       , with some additional volatile ices such as
       <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">
        ammonia
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kuskov2005_20-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Kuskov2005-20">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The mass fraction of ices is 49–55%.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kuskov2005_20-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Kuskov2005-20">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spohn_2003_27-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Spohn_2003-27">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The exact composition of Callisto's
       <a href="/wiki/Rock_(geology)" title="Rock (geology)">
        rock
       </a>
       component is not known, but is probably close to the composition of L/LL type
       <a href="/wiki/Ordinary_chondrite" title="Ordinary chondrite">
        ordinary chondrites
       </a>
       , which are characterized by less total
       <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">
        iron
       </a>
       , less metallic iron and more
       <a href="/wiki/Iron_oxide" title="Iron oxide">
        iron oxide
       </a>
       than
       <a href="/wiki/H_chondrite" title="H chondrite">
        H chondrites
       </a>
       . The weight ratio of iron to
       <a href="/wiki/Silicon" title="Silicon">
        silicon
       </a>
       is 0.9–1.3 in Callisto, whereas the
       <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">
        solar ratio
       </a>
       is around 1:8.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kuskov2005_20-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Kuskov2005-20">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Callisto's surface has an
       <a href="/wiki/Albedo" title="Albedo">
        albedo
       </a>
       of about 20%.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moore2004_11-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Moore2004-11">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Its surface composition is thought to be broadly similar to its composition as a whole. Near-infrared
       <a href="/wiki/Spectroscopy" title="Spectroscopy">
        spectroscopy
       </a>
       has revealed the presence of water ice
       <a href="/wiki/Absorption_band" title="Absorption band">
        absorption bands
       </a>
       at wavelengths of 1.04, 1.25, 1.5, 2.0 and 3.0 micrometers.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moore2004_11-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-Moore2004-11">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Water ice seems to be ubiquitous on the surface of Callisto, with a mass fraction of 25–50%.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Showman1999_9-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Showman1999-9">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The analysis of high-resolution,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Near-infrared" title="Near-infrared">
        near-infrared
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">
        UV
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Spectrum" title="Spectrum">
        spectra
       </a>
       obtained by the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Galileo_(spacecraft)" title="Galileo (spacecraft)">
         Galileo
        </a>
       </i>
       spacecraft and from the ground has revealed various non-ice materials:
       <a href="/wiki/Magnesium" title="Magnesium">
        magnesium
       </a>
       - and
       <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">
        iron
       </a>
       -bearing hydrated
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Silicates" title="Silicates">
        silicates
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moore2004_11-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-Moore2004-11">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">
        carbon dioxide
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown2003_39-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Brown2003-39">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_dioxide" title="Sulfur dioxide">
        sulfur dioxide
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Noll1996_40-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Noll1996-40">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and possibly
       <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">
        ammonia
       </a>
       and various
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organic_compounds" title="Organic compounds">
        organic compounds
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Showman1999_9-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Showman1999-9">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moore2004_11-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-Moore2004-11">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Spectral data indicate that Callisto's surface is extremely heterogeneous at the small scale. Small, bright patches of pure water ice are intermixed with patches of a rock–ice mixture and extended dark areas made of a non-ice material.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moore2004_11-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-Moore2004-11">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greeley_2000_23-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Greeley_2000-23">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The Callistoan surface is asymmetric: the leading hemisphere
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-footnote2_41-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-footnote2-41">
         [g]
        </a>
       </sup>
       is darker than the trailing one. This is different from other
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galilean_satellites" title="Galilean satellites">
        Galilean satellites
       </a>
       , where the reverse is true.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moore2004_11-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-Moore2004-11">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The trailing hemisphere
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-footnote2_41-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-footnote2-41">
         [g]
        </a>
       </sup>
       of Callisto appears to be enriched in
       <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">
        carbon dioxide
       </a>
       , whereas the leading hemisphere has more
       <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_dioxide" title="Sulfur dioxide">
        sulfur dioxide
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hibbitts1998_42-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Hibbitts1998-42">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Many fresh
       <a href="/wiki/Impact_crater" title="Impact crater">
        impact craters
       </a>
       like
       <a href="/wiki/Lofn_(crater)" title="Lofn (crater)">
        Lofn
       </a>
       also show enrichment in carbon dioxide.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hibbitts1998_42-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Hibbitts1998-42">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Overall, the chemical composition of the surface, especially in the dark areas, may be close to that seen on
       <a href="/wiki/D-type_asteroid" title="D-type asteroid">
        D-type asteroids
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greeley_2000_23-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Greeley_2000-23">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       whose surfaces are made of
       <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">
        carbonaceous
       </a>
       material.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Internal_structure">
        Internal structure
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Callisto_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Internal structure">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA01478_Interior_of_Callisto.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1700" data-file-width="2220" height="168" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/PIA01478_Interior_of_Callisto.jpg/220px-PIA01478_Interior_of_Callisto.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/PIA01478_Interior_of_Callisto.jpg/330px-PIA01478_Interior_of_Callisto.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/PIA01478_Interior_of_Callisto.jpg/440px-PIA01478_Interior_of_Callisto.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA01478_Interior_of_Callisto.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Model of Callisto's internal structure showing a surface ice layer, a possible liquid water layer, and an ice–rock interior
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Callisto's battered surface lies on top of a cold, stiff, and icy
       <a href="/wiki/Lithosphere" title="Lithosphere">
        lithosphere
       </a>
       that is between 80 and 150 km thick.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kuskov2005_20-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-Kuskov2005-20">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spohn_2003_27-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Spohn_2003-27">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A salty ocean 150–200 km deep may lie beneath the
       <a href="/wiki/Crust_(geology)" title="Crust (geology)">
        crust
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kuskov2005_20-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-Kuskov2005-20">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spohn_2003_27-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Spohn_2003-27">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
       indicated by studies of the
       <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">
        magnetic fields
       </a>
       around Jupiter and its moons.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Khurana_2000_43-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Khurana_2000-43">
         [36]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zimmer_2000_44-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Zimmer_2000-44">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It was found that Callisto responds to Jupiter's varying background magnetic field like a perfectly
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_conductivity" title="Electrical conductivity">
        conducting
       </a>
       sphere; that is, the field cannot penetrate inside Callisto, suggesting a layer of highly conductive fluid within it with a thickness of at least 10 km.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zimmer_2000_44-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Zimmer_2000-44">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The existence of an ocean is more likely if water contains a small amount of
       <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">
        ammonia
       </a>
       or other
       <a href="/wiki/Antifreeze" title="Antifreeze">
        antifreeze
       </a>
       , up to 5% by weight.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spohn_2003_27-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-Spohn_2003-27">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In this case the water+ice layer can be as thick as 250–300 km.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kuskov2005_20-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-Kuskov2005-20">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Failing an ocean, the icy lithosphere may be somewhat thicker, up to about 300 km.
      </p>
      <p>
       Beneath the lithosphere and putative ocean, Callisto's interior appears to be neither entirely uniform nor particularly variable.
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Galileo_(spacecraft)" title="Galileo (spacecraft)">
         Galileo
        </a>
       </i>
       orbiter data
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Anderson_2001_5-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-Anderson_2001-5">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       (especially the dimensionless
       <a href="/wiki/Moment_of_inertia" title="Moment of inertia">
        moment of inertia
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
        <a href="#cite_note-45">
         [h]
        </a>
       </sup>
       —0.3549 ± 0.0042—determined during close flybys) suggest that its interior is composed of compressed
       <a href="/wiki/Rock_(geology)" title="Rock (geology)">
        rocks
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Ice" title="Ice">
        ices
       </a>
       , with the amount of rock increasing with depth due to partial settling of its constituents.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kuskov2005_20-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-Kuskov2005-20">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Anderson_1998_46-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Anderson_1998-46">
         [38]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In other words, Callisto is only partially
       <a href="/wiki/Planetary_differentiation" title="Planetary differentiation">
        differentiated
       </a>
       . The density and moment of inertia are compatible with the existence of a small
       <a href="/wiki/Silicate" title="Silicate">
        silicate
       </a>
       core in the center of Callisto. The radius of any such core cannot exceed 600 km, and the density may lie between 3.1 and 3.6 g/cm
       <sup>
        3
       </sup>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Anderson_2001_5-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-Anderson_2001-5">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kuskov2005_20-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-Kuskov2005-20">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Callisto's interior is in stark contrast to
       <a href="/wiki/Ganymede_(moon)#Internal_structure" title="Ganymede (moon)">
        that of Ganymede
       </a>
       , which appears to be fully differentiated.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Showman1999_9-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-Showman1999-9">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sohl2002_47-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Sohl2002-47">
         [39]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Surface_features">
        Surface features
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Callisto_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Surface features">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_geological_features_on_Callisto" title="List of geological features on Callisto">
        List of geological features on Callisto
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cratered_plains_PIA00745.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2231" data-file-width="1691" height="224" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Cratered_plains_PIA00745.jpg/170px-Cratered_plains_PIA00745.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Cratered_plains_PIA00745.jpg/255px-Cratered_plains_PIA00745.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Cratered_plains_PIA00745.jpg/340px-Cratered_plains_PIA00745.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cratered_plains_PIA00745.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <i>
          Galileo
         </i>
         image of cratered plains, illustrating the pervasive local smoothing of Callisto's surface
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The ancient surface of Callisto is one of the most heavily cratered in the Solar System.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zahnle_1998_48-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Zahnle_1998-48">
         [40]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In fact, the
       <a href="/wiki/Impact_crater" title="Impact crater">
        crater
       </a>
       density is close to
       <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/saturation" title="wikt:saturation">
        saturation
       </a>
       : any new crater will tend to erase an older one. The large-scale
       <a href="/wiki/Geology" title="Geology">
        geology
       </a>
       is relatively simple; there are no large mountains on Callisto, volcanoes or other
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endogeny" title="Endogeny">
        endogenic
       </a>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tectonic" title="Tectonic">
        tectonic
       </a>
       features.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bender_1997_49-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Bender_1997-49">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The impact craters and multi-ring structures—together with associated
       <a href="/wiki/Fracture_(geology)" title="Fracture (geology)">
        fractures
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Escarpment" title="Escarpment">
        scarps
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deposit_(geology)" title="Deposit (geology)">
        deposits
       </a>
       —are the only large features to be found on the surface.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greeley_2000_23-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-Greeley_2000-23">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bender_1997_49-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Bender_1997-49">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Callisto's surface can be divided into several geologically different parts: cratered plains, light plains, bright and dark smooth plains, and various units associated with particular multi-ring structures and impact craters.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greeley_2000_23-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-Greeley_2000-23">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bender_1997_49-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Bender_1997-49">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The cratered plains constitute most of the surface area and represent the ancient lithosphere, a mixture of ice and rocky material. The light plains include bright impact craters like
       <a href="/wiki/Asgard_(crater)" title="Asgard (crater)">
        Burr
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Lofn_(crater)" title="Lofn (crater)">
        Lofn
       </a>
       , as well as the effaced remnants of old large craters called
       <a href="/wiki/Palimpsest_(planetary_astronomy)" title="Palimpsest (planetary astronomy)">
        palimpsests
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
        <a href="#cite_note-50">
         [i]
        </a>
       </sup>
       the central parts of multi-ring structures, and isolated patches in the cratered plains.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greeley_2000_23-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-Greeley_2000-23">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       These light plains are thought to be icy impact deposits. The bright, smooth plains constitute a small fraction of Callisto's surface and are found in the ridge and
       <a href="/wiki/Trough_(geology)" title="Trough (geology)">
        trough
       </a>
       zones of the
       <a href="/wiki/Valhalla_(crater)" title="Valhalla (crater)">
        Valhalla
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Asgard_(crater)" title="Asgard (crater)">
        Asgard
       </a>
       formations and as isolated spots in the cratered plains. They were thought to be connected with
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endogenic" title="Endogenic">
        endogenic
       </a>
       activity, but the high-resolution
       <i>
        Galileo
       </i>
       images showed that the bright, smooth plains correlate with heavily fractured and knobby terrain and do not show any signs of resurfacing.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greeley_2000_23-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-Greeley_2000-23">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The
       <i>
        Galileo
       </i>
       images also revealed small, dark, smooth areas with overall coverage less than 10,000 km
       <sup>
        2
       </sup>
       , which appear to embay
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
        <a href="#cite_note-51">
         [j]
        </a>
       </sup>
       the surrounding terrain. They are possible
       <a href="/wiki/Cryovolcano" title="Cryovolcano">
        cryovolcanic
       </a>
       deposits.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greeley_2000_23-9">
        <a href="#cite_note-Greeley_2000-23">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Both the light and the various smooth plains are somewhat younger and less cratered than the background cratered plains.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greeley_2000_23-10">
        <a href="#cite_note-Greeley_2000-23">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wagner_2001_52-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Wagner_2001-52">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Callisto_Har_PIA01054.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3320" data-file-width="3600" height="203" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Callisto_Har_PIA01054.jpg/220px-Callisto_Har_PIA01054.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Callisto_Har_PIA01054.jpg/330px-Callisto_Har_PIA01054.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Callisto_Har_PIA01054.jpg/440px-Callisto_Har_PIA01054.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Callisto_Har_PIA01054.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Impact crater
         <a href="/wiki/H%C3%A1r_(crater)" title="Hár (crater)">
          Hár
         </a>
         with a central dome.
         <a href="/wiki/Crater_chain" title="Crater chain">
          Chains
         </a>
         of
         <a href="/wiki/Secondary_crater" title="Secondary crater">
          secondary craters
         </a>
         from formation of the more recent crater
         <a href="/wiki/Tindr_(crater)" title="Tindr (crater)">
          Tindr
         </a>
         at upper right crosscut the terrain.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Impact crater diameters seen range from 0.1 km—a limit defined by the
       <a href="/wiki/Image_resolution" title="Image resolution">
        imaging resolution
       </a>
       —to over 100 km, not counting the multi-ring structures.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greeley_2000_23-11">
        <a href="#cite_note-Greeley_2000-23">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Small craters, with diameters less than 5 km, have simple bowl or flat-floored shapes. Those 5–40 km across usually have a central peak. Larger impact features, with diameters in the range 25–100 km, have central pits instead of peaks, such as
       <a href="/wiki/Tindr_(crater)" title="Tindr (crater)">
        Tindr
       </a>
       crater.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greeley_2000_23-12">
        <a href="#cite_note-Greeley_2000-23">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The largest craters with diameters over 60 km can have central domes, which are thought to result from central
       <a href="/wiki/Tectonic_uplift" title="Tectonic uplift">
        tectonic uplift
       </a>
       after an impact;
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greeley_2000_23-13">
        <a href="#cite_note-Greeley_2000-23">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       examples include
       <a href="/wiki/Asgard_(crater)" title="Asgard (crater)">
        Doh
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/H%C3%A1r_(crater)" title="Hár (crater)">
        Hár
       </a>
       craters. A small number of very large—more 100 km in diameter—and bright impact craters show anomalous dome geometry. These are unusually shallow and may be a transitional
       <a href="/wiki/Landform" title="Landform">
        landform
       </a>
       to the multi-ring structures, as with the
       <a href="/wiki/Lofn_(crater)" title="Lofn (crater)">
        Lofn
       </a>
       impact feature.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greeley_2000_23-14">
        <a href="#cite_note-Greeley_2000-23">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Callisto's craters are generally shallower than those on the
       <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">
        Moon
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Valhalla_crater_on_Callisto.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="714" data-file-width="1196" height="131" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Valhalla_crater_on_Callisto.jpg/220px-Valhalla_crater_on_Callisto.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Valhalla_crater_on_Callisto.jpg/330px-Valhalla_crater_on_Callisto.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Valhalla_crater_on_Callisto.jpg/440px-Valhalla_crater_on_Callisto.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Valhalla_crater_on_Callisto.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <i>
          <a href="/wiki/Voyager_1" title="Voyager 1">
           Voyager 1
          </a>
         </i>
         image of
         <a href="/wiki/Valhalla_(crater)" title="Valhalla (crater)">
          Valhalla
         </a>
         , a
         <a href="/wiki/Complex_crater" title="Complex crater">
          multi-ring impact structure
         </a>
         3800 km in diameter
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The largest impact features on Callisto's surface are multi-ring basins.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greeley_2000_23-15">
        <a href="#cite_note-Greeley_2000-23">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bender_1997_49-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Bender_1997-49">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Two are enormous.
       <a href="/wiki/Valhalla_(crater)" title="Valhalla (crater)">
        Valhalla
       </a>
       is the largest, with a bright central region 600 kilometers in diameter, and rings extending as far as 1,800 kilometers from the center (see figure).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Map_2002_53-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Map_2002-53">
         [43]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The second largest is
       <a href="/wiki/Asgard_(crater)" title="Asgard (crater)">
        Asgard
       </a>
       , measuring about 1,600 kilometers in diameter.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Map_2002_53-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Map_2002-53">
         [43]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Multi-ring structures probably originated as a result of a post-impact
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Concentric" title="Concentric">
        concentric
       </a>
       fracturing of the lithosphere lying on a layer of soft or liquid material, possibly an ocean.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Klemaszewski2001_33-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Klemaszewski2001-33">
         [27]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The catenae—for example
       <a href="/wiki/Gomul_Catena" title="Gomul Catena">
        Gomul Catena
       </a>
       —are long chains of impact craters lined up in straight lines across the surface. They were probably created by objects that were tidally disrupted as they passed close to Jupiter prior to the impact on Callisto, or by very
       <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/oblique" title="wikt:oblique">
        oblique
       </a>
       impacts.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greeley_2000_23-16">
        <a href="#cite_note-Greeley_2000-23">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A historical example of a disruption was
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Comet_Shoemaker-Levy_9" title="Comet Shoemaker-Levy 9">
        Comet Shoemaker-Levy 9
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       As mentioned above, small patches of pure water ice with an
       <a href="/wiki/Albedo" title="Albedo">
        albedo
       </a>
       as high as 80% are found on the surface of Callisto, surrounded by much darker material.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moore2004_11-9">
        <a href="#cite_note-Moore2004-11">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       High-resolution
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Galileo_(spacecraft)" title="Galileo (spacecraft)">
         Galileo
        </a>
       </i>
       images showed the bright patches to be predominately located on elevated surface features:
       <a href="/wiki/Rim_(craters)" title="Rim (craters)">
        crater rims
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Escarpment" title="Escarpment">
        scarps
       </a>
       , ridges and knobs.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moore2004_11-10">
        <a href="#cite_note-Moore2004-11">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       They are likely to be thin
       <a href="/wiki/Frost" title="Frost">
        water frost
       </a>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deposit_(geology)" title="Deposit (geology)">
        deposits
       </a>
       . Dark material usually lies in the lowlands surrounding and mantling bright features and appears to be smooth. It often forms patches up to 5 km across within the crater floors and in the intercrater depressions.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moore2004_11-11">
        <a href="#cite_note-Moore2004-11">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Landslides_and_knobs_PIA01095.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2200" data-file-width="1760" height="213" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Landslides_and_knobs_PIA01095.jpg/170px-Landslides_and_knobs_PIA01095.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Landslides_and_knobs_PIA01095.jpg/255px-Landslides_and_knobs_PIA01095.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Landslides_and_knobs_PIA01095.jpg/340px-Landslides_and_knobs_PIA01095.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Landslides_and_knobs_PIA01095.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Two
         <a href="/wiki/Landslide" title="Landslide">
          landslides
         </a>
         3–3.5 km long are visible on the right sides of the floors of the two large craters on the right.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       On a sub-kilometer scale the surface of Callisto is more degraded than the surfaces of other icy
       <a href="/wiki/Galilean_moons" title="Galilean moons">
        Galilean moons
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moore2004_11-12">
        <a href="#cite_note-Moore2004-11">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Typically there is a deficit of small impact craters with diameters less than 1 km as compared with, for instance, the dark plains on
       <a href="/wiki/Ganymede_(moon)" title="Ganymede (moon)">
        Ganymede
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greeley_2000_23-17">
        <a href="#cite_note-Greeley_2000-23">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Instead of small craters, the almost ubiquitous surface features are small knobs and pits.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moore2004_11-13">
        <a href="#cite_note-Moore2004-11">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The knobs are thought to represent remnants of crater rims degraded by an as-yet uncertain process.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moore1999_24-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Moore1999-24">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The most likely candidate process is the slow
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sublimation_(chemistry)" title="Sublimation (chemistry)">
        sublimation
       </a>
       of ice, which is enabled by a temperature of up to 165
       <a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">
        K
       </a>
       , reached at a subsolar point.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moore2004_11-14">
        <a href="#cite_note-Moore2004-11">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Such sublimation of water or other
       <a href="/wiki/Volatiles" title="Volatiles">
        volatiles
       </a>
       from the dirty ice that is the
       <a href="/wiki/Bedrock" title="Bedrock">
        bedrock
       </a>
       causes its decomposition. The non-ice remnants form
       <a href="/wiki/Debris" title="Debris">
        debris
       </a>
       avalanches descending from the slopes of the crater walls.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moore1999_24-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Moore1999-24">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Such avalanches are often observed near and inside impact craters and termed "debris aprons".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moore2004_11-15">
        <a href="#cite_note-Moore2004-11">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greeley_2000_23-18">
        <a href="#cite_note-Greeley_2000-23">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moore1999_24-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-Moore1999-24">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Sometimes crater walls are cut by sinuous valley-like incisions called "gullies", which resemble certain
       <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">
        Martian
       </a>
       surface features.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moore2004_11-16">
        <a href="#cite_note-Moore2004-11">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In the ice sublimation hypothesis, the low-lying dark material is interpreted as a blanket of primarily non-ice debris, which originated from the degraded rims of craters and has covered a predominantly icy bedrock.
      </p>
      <p>
       The relative ages of the different surface units on Callisto can be determined from the density of impact craters on them. The older the surface, the denser the crater population.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chapman1997_54-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Chapman1997-54">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Absolute dating has not been carried out, but based on theoretical considerations, the cratered plains are thought to be ~4.5
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1000000000_(number)" title="1000000000 (number)">
        billion
       </a>
       years old, dating back almost to the formation of the
       <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">
        Solar System
       </a>
       . The ages of multi-ring structures and impact craters depend on chosen background cratering rates and are estimated by different authors to vary between 1 and 4 billion years.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greeley_2000_23-19">
        <a href="#cite_note-Greeley_2000-23">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zahnle_1998_48-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Zahnle_1998-48">
         [40]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Atmosphere_and_ionosphere">
        Atmosphere and ionosphere
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Callisto_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Atmosphere and ionosphere">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Callisto_field.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="757" data-file-width="801" height="208" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Callisto_field.svg/220px-Callisto_field.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Callisto_field.svg/330px-Callisto_field.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Callisto_field.svg/440px-Callisto_field.svg.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Callisto_field.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Induced magnetic field around Callisto
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Callisto has a very tenuous atmosphere composed of
       <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">
        carbon dioxide
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carlson_1999_13-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Carlson_1999-13">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It was detected by the
       <i>
        Galileo
       </i>
       Near Infrared Mapping Spectrometer (NIMS) from its absorption feature near the wavelength 4.2
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Micrometers" title="Micrometers">
        micrometers
       </a>
       . The surface pressure is estimated to be 7.5  × 10
       <sup>
        −12
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Bar_(unit)" title="Bar (unit)">
        bar
       </a>
       (0.75
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Micropascal" title="Micropascal">
        µPa
       </a>
       ) and particle density 4 × 10
       <sup>
        8
       </sup>
       cm
       <sup>
        −3
       </sup>
       . Because such a thin atmosphere would be lost in only about 4 days
       <i>
        (see
        <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_escape" title="Atmospheric escape">
         atmospheric escape
        </a>
        )
       </i>
       , it must be constantly replenished, possibly by slow sublimation of carbon dioxide ice from Callisto's icy crust,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carlson_1999_13-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-Carlson_1999-13">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       which would be compatible with the sublimation–degradation hypothesis for the formation of the surface knobs.
      </p>
      <p>
       Callisto's ionosphere was first detected during
       <i>
        Galileo
       </i>
       flybys;
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kliore_2002_25-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Kliore_2002-25">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
       its high electron density of 7–17 × 10
       <sup>
        4
       </sup>
       cm
       <sup>
        −3
       </sup>
       cannot be explained by the photoionization of the atmospheric
       <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">
        carbon dioxide
       </a>
       alone. Hence, it is suspected that the atmosphere of Callisto is actually dominated by
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Molecular_oxygen" title="Molecular oxygen">
        molecular oxygen
       </a>
       (in amounts 10–100 times greater than
       <span class="chemf nowrap">
        CO
        <span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left">
         <br/>
         2
        </span>
       </span>
       ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Liang_2005_14-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Liang_2005-14">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However,
       <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">
        oxygen
       </a>
       has not yet been directly detected in the atmosphere of Callisto. Observations with the
       <a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">
        Hubble Space Telescope
       </a>
       (HST) placed an upper limit on its possible concentration in the atmosphere, based on lack of detection, which is still compatible with the ionospheric measurements.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Strobel2002_55-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Strobel2002-55">
         [45]
        </a>
       </sup>
       At the same time, HST was able to detect
       <a href="/wiki/Condensation" title="Condensation">
        condensed
       </a>
       oxygen trapped on the surface of Callisto.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spencer2002_56-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Spencer2002-56">
         [46]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Atomic hydrogen has also been detected in Callisto's atmosphere via recent analysis of 2001 Hubble Space Telescope data.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_57-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-:0-57">
         [47]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Spectral images taken on 15 and 24 December 2001 were re-examined, revealing a faint signal of scattered light that indicates a hydrogen corona. The observed brightness from the scattered sunlight in Callisto's hydrogen corona is approximately two times larger when the leading hemisphere is observed. This asymmetry may originate from a different hydrogen abundance in both leading and trailing hemispheres. However, this hemispheric difference in Callisto's hydrogen corona brightness is likely to originate from the extinction of the signal in the Earth's
       <a href="/wiki/Geocorona" title="Geocorona">
        geocorona
       </a>
       , which is greater when the trailing hemisphere is observed
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58">
        <a href="#cite_note-58">
         [48]
        </a>
       </sup>
       .
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Origin_and_evolution">
        Origin and evolution
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Callisto_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Origin and evolution">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       The partial
       <a href="/wiki/Planetary_differentiation" title="Planetary differentiation">
        differentiation
       </a>
       of Callisto (inferred e.g. from moment of inertia measurements) means that it has never been heated enough to melt its ice component.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spohn_2003_27-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-Spohn_2003-27">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Therefore, the most favorable model of its formation is a slow
       <a href="/wiki/Accretion_(astrophysics)" title="Accretion (astrophysics)">
        accretion
       </a>
       in the low-density Jovian
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_nebula" title="Solar nebula">
        subnebula
       </a>
       —a disk of the gas and dust that existed around Jupiter after its formation.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Canup2002_26-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Canup2002-26">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
