       Such a prolonged accretion stage would allow cooling to largely keep up with the heat accumulation caused by impacts, radioactive decay and contraction, thereby preventing melting and fast differentiation.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Canup2002_26-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Canup2002-26">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The allowable timescale of formation of Callisto lies then in the range 0.1 million–10 million years.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Canup2002_26-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Canup2002-26">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jagged_Hills_PIA03455.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1510" data-file-width="2000" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Jagged_Hills_PIA03455.jpg/220px-Jagged_Hills_PIA03455.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Jagged_Hills_PIA03455.jpg/330px-Jagged_Hills_PIA03455.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Jagged_Hills_PIA03455.jpg/440px-Jagged_Hills_PIA03455.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jagged_Hills_PIA03455.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Views of eroding (top) and mostly eroded (bottom) ice knobs (~100 m high), possibly formed from the
         <a href="/wiki/Ejecta_blanket" title="Ejecta blanket">
          ejecta
         </a>
         of an ancient
         <a href="/wiki/Impact_crater" title="Impact crater">
          impact
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The further evolution of Callisto after
       <a href="/wiki/Accretion_(astrophysics)" title="Accretion (astrophysics)">
        accretion
       </a>
       was determined by the balance of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radioactive" title="Radioactive">
        radioactive
       </a>
       heating, cooling through
       <a href="/wiki/Thermal_conduction" title="Thermal conduction">
        thermal conduction
       </a>
       near the surface, and solid state or subsolidus
       <a href="/wiki/Convection" title="Convection">
        convection
       </a>
       in the interior.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Freeman2006_35-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Freeman2006-35">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Details of the subsolidus convection in the ice is the main source of uncertainty in the models of all
       <a href="/wiki/Icy_moon" title="Icy moon">
        icy moons
       </a>
       . It is known to develop when the temperature is sufficiently close to the
       <a href="/wiki/Melting_point" title="Melting point">
        melting point
       </a>
       , due to the temperature dependence of ice
       <a href="/wiki/Viscosity" title="Viscosity">
        viscosity
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McKinnon2006_59-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-McKinnon2006-59">
         [49]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Subsolidus convection in icy bodies is a slow process with ice motions of the order of 1 centimeter per year, but is, in fact, a very effective cooling mechanism on long timescales.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McKinnon2006_59-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-McKinnon2006-59">
         [49]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It is thought to proceed in the so-called stagnant lid regime, where a stiff, cold outer layer of Callisto conducts heat without convection, whereas the ice beneath it convects in the subsolidus regime.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spohn_2003_27-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-Spohn_2003-27">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McKinnon2006_59-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-McKinnon2006-59">
         [49]
        </a>
       </sup>
       For Callisto, the outer conductive layer corresponds to the cold and rigid
       <a href="/wiki/Lithosphere" title="Lithosphere">
        lithosphere
       </a>
       with a thickness of about 100 km. Its presence would explain the lack of any signs of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endogenic" title="Endogenic">
        endogenic
       </a>
       activity on the Callistoan surface.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McKinnon2006_59-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-McKinnon2006-59">
         [49]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nagel2004_60-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Nagel2004-60">
         [50]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The convection in the interior parts of Callisto may be layered, because under the high pressures found there,
       <a href="/wiki/Ice" title="Ice">
        water ice
       </a>
       exists in different crystalline phases beginning from the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ice_I" title="Ice I">
        ice I
       </a>
       on the surface to
       <a href="/wiki/Ice_VII" title="Ice VII">
        ice VII
       </a>
       in the center.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Freeman2006_35-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Freeman2006-35">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The early onset of subsolidus convection in the Callistoan interior could have prevented large-scale ice melting and any resulting
       <a href="/wiki/Planetary_differentiation" title="Planetary differentiation">
        differentiation
       </a>
       that would have otherwise formed a large rocky
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Core_(geology)" title="Core (geology)">
        core
       </a>
       and icy
       <a href="/wiki/Mantle_(geology)" title="Mantle (geology)">
        mantle
       </a>
       . Due to the convection process, however, very slow and partial separation and differentiation of rocks and ices inside Callisto has been proceeding on timescales of billions of years and may be continuing to this day.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nagel2004_60-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Nagel2004-60">
         [50]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The current understanding of the evolution of Callisto allows for the existence of a layer or "ocean" of liquid water in its interior. This is connected with the anomalous behavior of ice I phase's melting temperature, which decreases with
       <a href="/wiki/Pressure" title="Pressure">
        pressure
       </a>
       , achieving temperatures as low as 251 K at 2,070 bar (207
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Megapascal" title="Megapascal">
        MPa
       </a>
       ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spohn_2003_27-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-Spohn_2003-27">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In all realistic models of Callisto the temperature in the layer between 100 and 200 km in depth is very close to, or exceeds slightly, this anomalous melting temperature.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Freeman2006_35-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Freeman2006-35">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McKinnon2006_59-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-McKinnon2006-59">
         [49]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nagel2004_60-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Nagel2004-60">
         [50]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The presence of even small amounts of
       <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">
        ammonia
       </a>
       —about 1–2% by weight—almost guarantees the liquid's existence because ammonia would lower the melting temperature even further.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spohn_2003_27-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-Spohn_2003-27">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Although Callisto is very similar in bulk properties to
       <a href="/wiki/Ganymede_(moon)" title="Ganymede (moon)">
        Ganymede
       </a>
       , it apparently had a much simpler
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geological_history" title="Geological history">
        geological history
       </a>
       . The surface appears to have been shaped mainly by impacts and other
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exogenic" title="Exogenic">
        exogenic
       </a>
       forces.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greeley_2000_23-20">
        <a href="#cite_note-Greeley_2000-23">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Unlike neighboring Ganymede with its grooved terrain, there is little evidence of
       <a href="/wiki/Plate_tectonics" title="Plate tectonics">
        tectonic
       </a>
       activity.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Showman1999_9-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-Showman1999-9">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Explanations that have been proposed for the contrasts in internal heating and consequent differentiation and geologic activity between Callisto and Ganymede include differences in formation conditions,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barr2_61-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Barr2-61">
         [51]
        </a>
       </sup>
       the greater tidal heating experienced by Ganymede,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Showman2_62-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Showman2-62">
         [52]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and the more numerous and energetic impacts that would have been suffered by Ganymede during the
       <a href="/wiki/Late_Heavy_Bombardment" title="Late Heavy Bombardment">
        Late Heavy Bombardment
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baldwin_63-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Baldwin-63">
         [53]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LPI1158_64-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-LPI1158-64">
         [54]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barr_65-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Barr-65">
         [55]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The relatively simple geological history of Callisto provides planetary scientists with a reference point for comparison with other more active and complex worlds.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Showman1999_9-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-Showman1999-9">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Potential_habitability">
        Potential habitability
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Callisto_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Potential habitability">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       It is speculated that there could be life in Callisto's subsurface ocean. Like
       <a href="/wiki/Europa_(moon)" title="Europa (moon)">
        Europa
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Ganymede_(moon)" title="Ganymede (moon)">
        Ganymede
       </a>
       , as well as
       <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">
        Saturn
       </a>
       's
       <a href="/wiki/Moons_of_Saturn" title="Moons of Saturn">
        moons
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Enceladus" title="Enceladus">
        Enceladus
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Mimas_(moon)" title="Mimas (moon)">
        Mimas
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Dione_(moon)" title="Dione (moon)">
        Dione
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Titan_(moon)" title="Titan (moon)">
        Titan
       </a>
       , a possible subsurface ocean might be composed of
       <a href="/wiki/Saline_water" title="Saline water">
        salt water
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       It is possible that
       <a href="/wiki/Halophile" title="Halophile">
        halophiles
       </a>
       could thrive in the ocean.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA_finds_ocean_66-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-NASA_finds_ocean-66">
         [56]
        </a>
       </sup>
       As with
       <a href="/wiki/Europa_(moon)" title="Europa (moon)">
        Europa
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Ganymede_(moon)" title="Ganymede (moon)">
        Ganymede
       </a>
       , the idea has been raised that
       <a href="/wiki/Planetary_habitability" title="Planetary habitability">
        habitable
       </a>
       conditions and even
       <a href="/wiki/Extraterrestrial_life" title="Extraterrestrial life">
        extraterrestrial microbial life
       </a>
       may exist in the salty
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Subsurface_ocean" title="Subsurface ocean">
        ocean
       </a>
       under the Callistoan surface.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lipps2004_28-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Lipps2004-28">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, the environmental conditions necessary for life appear to be less favorable on Callisto than on Europa. The principal reasons are the lack of contact with rocky material and the lower heat flux from the interior of Callisto.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lipps2004_28-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Lipps2004-28">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Scientist Torrence Johnson said the following about comparing the odds of life on Callisto with the odds on other
       <a href="/wiki/Galilean_moons" title="Galilean moons">
        Galilean moons
       </a>
       :
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Phillips_67-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Phillips-67">
         [57]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <blockquote>
       <p>
        The basic ingredients for life—what we call 'pre-biotic chemistry'—are abundant in many solar system objects, such as comets, asteroids and icy moons. Biologists believe liquid water and energy are then needed to actually support life, so it's exciting to find another place where we might have liquid water. But, energy is another matter, and currently, Callisto's ocean is only being heated by radioactive elements, whereas Europa has tidal energy as well, from its greater proximity to Jupiter.
       </p>
      </blockquote>
      <p>
       Based on the considerations mentioned above and on other scientific observations, it is thought that of all of Jupiter's moons, Europa has the greatest chance of supporting
       <a href="/wiki/Microorganism" title="Microorganism">
        microbial life
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lipps2004_28-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Lipps2004-28">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-François2005_68-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-François2005-68">
         [58]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Exploration">
        Exploration
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Callisto_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Exploration">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       The
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Pioneer_10" title="Pioneer 10">
         Pioneer 10
        </a>
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Pioneer_11" title="Pioneer 11">
         Pioneer 11
        </a>
       </i>
       Jupiter encounters in the early 1970s contributed little new information about Callisto in comparison with what was already known from Earth-based observations.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moore2004_11-17">
        <a href="#cite_note-Moore2004-11">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The real breakthrough happened later with the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Voyager_1" title="Voyager 1">
         Voyager 1
        </a>
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Voyager_2" title="Voyager 2">
         Voyager 2
        </a>
       </i>
       flybys in 1979. They imaged more than half of the Callistoan surface with a resolution of 1–2 km, and precisely measured its temperature, mass and shape.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moore2004_11-18">
        <a href="#cite_note-Moore2004-11">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A second round of exploration lasted from 1994 to 2003, when the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Galileo_(spacecraft)" title="Galileo (spacecraft)">
         Galileo
        </a>
       </i>
       spacecraft had eight close encounters with Callisto, the last flyby during the C30 orbit in 2001 came as close as 138 km to the surface. The
       <i>
        Galileo
       </i>
       orbiter completed the global imaging of the surface and delivered a number of pictures with a resolution as high as 15 meters of selected areas of Callisto.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greeley_2000_23-21">
        <a href="#cite_note-Greeley_2000-23">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 2000, the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Cassini%E2%80%93Huygens" title="Cassini–Huygens">
         Cassini
        </a>
       </i>
       spacecraft en route to
       <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">
        Saturn
       </a>
       acquired high-quality infrared spectra of the Galilean satellites including Callisto.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown2003_39-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Brown2003-39">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In February–March 2007, the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/New_Horizons" title="New Horizons">
         New Horizons
        </a>
       </i>
       probe on its way to Pluto obtained new images and spectra of Callisto.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morring2007_69-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Morring2007-69">
         [59]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The next planned mission to the Jovian system is the
       <a href="/wiki/European_Space_Agency" title="European Space Agency">
        European Space Agency
       </a>
       's
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jupiter_Icy_Moon_Explorer" title="Jupiter Icy Moon Explorer">
        Jupiter Icy Moon Explorer
       </a>
       (JUICE), due to launch in 2022.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-selection_70-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-selection-70">
         [60]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Several close flybys of Callisto are planned during the mission.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-selection_70-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-selection-70">
         [60]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Old_proposals">
        Old proposals
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Callisto_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Old proposals">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Formerly proposed for a launch in 2020, the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Europa_Jupiter_System_Mission" title="Europa Jupiter System Mission">
        Europa Jupiter System Mission
       </a>
       (EJSM) was a joint
       <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">
        NASA
       </a>
       /
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ESA" title="ESA">
        ESA
       </a>
       proposal for exploration of
       <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">
        Jupiter
       </a>
       's moons. In February 2009 it was announced that ESA/NASA had given this mission priority ahead of the
       <a href="/wiki/Titan_Saturn_System_Mission" title="Titan Saturn System Mission">
        Titan Saturn System Mission
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71">
        <a href="#cite_note-71">
         [61]
        </a>
       </sup>
       ESA's contribution still faced funding competition from other ESA projects.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72">
        <a href="#cite_note-72">
         [62]
        </a>
       </sup>
       EJSM consisted of the NASA-led
       <a href="/wiki/Jupiter_Europa_Orbiter" title="Jupiter Europa Orbiter">
        Jupiter Europa Orbiter
       </a>
       , the ESA-led
       <a href="/wiki/Jupiter_Ganymede_Orbiter" title="Jupiter Ganymede Orbiter">
        Jupiter Ganymede Orbiter
       </a>
       , and possibly a
       <a href="/wiki/JAXA" title="JAXA">
        JAXA
       </a>
       -led
       <a href="/wiki/Jupiter_Magnetospheric_Orbiter" title="Jupiter Magnetospheric Orbiter">
        Jupiter Magnetospheric Orbiter
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Potential_colonization">
        Potential colonization
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Callisto_(moon)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Potential colonization">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Callisto_base.PNG">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="720" data-file-width="1082" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Callisto_base.PNG/220px-Callisto_base.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Callisto_base.PNG/330px-Callisto_base.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Callisto_base.PNG/440px-Callisto_base.PNG 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Callisto_base.PNG" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Artist's impression of a base on Callisto
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CallistoBase_73-0">
          <a href="#cite_note-CallistoBase-73">
           [63]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       In 2003
       <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">
        NASA
       </a>
       conducted a conceptual study called
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Human_Outer_Planets_Exploration&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Human Outer Planets Exploration (page does not exist)">
        Human Outer Planets Exploration
       </a>
       (HOPE) regarding the future human exploration of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Outer_Solar_System" title="Outer Solar System">
        outer Solar System
       </a>
       . The target chosen to consider in detail was Callisto.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HOPE_29-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-HOPE-29">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74">
        <a href="#cite_note-74">
         [64]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The study proposed a possible surface base on Callisto that would produce
       <a href="/wiki/Rocket_propellant" title="Rocket propellant">
        rocket propellant
       </a>
       for further exploration of the Solar System.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CallistoBase_73-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-CallistoBase-73">
         [63]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Advantages of a base on Callisto include low radiation (due to its distance from Jupiter) and geological stability. Such a base could facilitate remote exploration of
       <a href="/wiki/Europa_(moon)" title="Europa (moon)">
        Europa
       </a>
       , or be an ideal location for a Jovian system waystation servicing spacecraft heading farther into the outer Solar System, using a
       <a href="/wiki/Gravity_assist" title="Gravity assist">
        gravity assist
       </a>
       from a close flyby of Jupiter after departing Callisto.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HOPE_29-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-HOPE-29">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In December 2003, NASA reported that a manned mission to Callisto might be possible in the 2040s.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75">
        <a href="#cite_note-75">
         [65]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>

        </span>

           <span class="mwe-math-element">
            <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
             <math alttext="{\displaystyle 4\pi r^{2}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
              <semantics>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
                 <mn>
                  4
                 </mn>
                 <mi>
                  π
                  <!-- π -->
                 </mi>
                 <msup>
                  <mi>
                   r
                  </mi>
                  <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                   <mn>
                    2
                   </mn>
                  </mrow>
                 </msup>
                </mstyle>
               </mrow>
               <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
                {\displaystyle 4\pi r^{2}}
               </annotation>
              </semantics>
             </math>
            </span>
            <img alt="4\pi r^{2}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/b81fcce302776a01dc66fc186a1ce0a616b4d772" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:4.597ex; height:2.676ex;"/>
           </span>
           .
          </span>
         </li>
         <li id="cite_note-7">
          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
           <b>
            <a href="#cite_ref-7">
             ^
            </a>
           </b>
          </span>
          <span class="reference-text">
           Volume derived from the radius (
           <i>
            r
           </i>
           ):
           <span class="mwe-math-element">
            <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
             <math alttext="{\displaystyle {\frac {4}{3}}\pi r^{3}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
              <semantics>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
                 <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                  <mfrac>
                   <mn>
                    4
                   </mn>
                   <mn>
                    3
                   </mn>
                  </mfrac>
                 </mrow>
                 <mi>
                  π
                  <!-- π -->
                 </mi>
                 <msup>
                  <mi>
                   r
                  </mi>
                  <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                   <mn>
                    3
                   </mn>
                  </mrow>
                 </msup>
                </mstyle>
               </mrow>
               <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
                {\displaystyle {\frac {4}{3}}\pi r^{3}}
               </annotation>
              </semantics>
             </math>
            </span>
            <img alt="{\frac {4}{3}}\pi r^{3}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/7112cb504e1399319cb9edbc622d1c4609fd56e3" style="vertical-align: -1.838ex; width:5.433ex; height:5.176ex;"/>
           </span>
           .
          </span>
         </li>
         <li id="cite_note-8">
          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
           <b>
            <a href="#cite_ref-8">
             ^
            </a>
           </b>
          </span>
          <span class="reference-text">
           Surface gravity derived from the mass (
           <i>
            m
           </i>
           ), the
           <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_constant" title="Gravitational constant">
            gravitational constant
           </a>
           (
           <i>
            G
           </i>
           ) and the radius (
           <i>
            r
           </i>
           ):
           <span class="mwe-math-element">
            <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
             <math alttext="{\displaystyle {\frac {Gm}{r^{2}}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
              <semantics>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
                 <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                  <mfrac>
                   <mrow>
                    <mi>
                     G
                    </mi>
                    <mi>
                     m
                    </mi>
                   </mrow>
                   <msup>
                    <mi>
                     r
                    </mi>
                    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                     <mn>
                      2
                     </mn>
                    </mrow>
                   </msup>
                  </mfrac>
                 </mrow>
                </mstyle>
               </mrow>
               <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
                {\displaystyle {\frac {Gm}{r^{2}}}}
               </annotation>
              </semantics>
             </math>
            </span>
            <img alt="{\frac  {Gm}{r^{2}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/3bc97461a59646cbc67b1bb4aec9323878ced62c" style="vertical-align: -2.171ex; width:4.703ex; height:5.676ex;"/>
           </span>
           .
          </span>
         </li>
         <li id="cite_note-10">
          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
           <b>
            <a href="#cite_ref-10">
             ^
            </a>
           </b>
          </span>
          <span class="reference-text">
           Escape velocity derived from the mass (
           <i>
            m
           </i>
           ), the
           <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_constant" title="Gravitational constant">
            gravitational constant
           </a>
           (
           <i>
            G
           </i>
           ) and the radius (
           <i>
            r
           </i>
           ):
           <span class="mwe-math-element">
            <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
             <math alttext="{\displaystyle \textstyle {\sqrt {\frac {2Gm}{r}}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
              <semantics>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
                 <mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">
                  <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                   <msqrt>
                    <mfrac>
                     <mrow>
                      <mn>
                       2
                      </mn>
                      <mi>
                       G
                      </mi>
                      <mi>
                       m
                      </mi>
                     </mrow>
                     <mi>
                      r
                     </mi>
                    </mfrac>
                   </msqrt>
                  </mrow>
                 </mstyle>
                </mstyle>
               </mrow>
               <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
                {\displaystyle \textstyle {\sqrt {\frac {2Gm}{r}}}}
               </annotation>
              </semantics>
             </math>
            </span>
            <img alt="\textstyle {\sqrt {\frac {2Gm}{r}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a58535443110131fc3db59b1b549eccac2f87149" style="vertical-align: -1.671ex; width:6.716ex; height:4.843ex;"/>
           </span>
           .
          </span>
         </li>
         <li id="cite_note-footnote2-41">
          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
           ^
           <a href="#cite_ref-footnote2_41-0">
            <sup>
             <i>
              <b>
               a
              </b>
             </i>
            </sup>
           </a>
           <a href="#cite_ref-footnote2_41-1">
            <sup>
             <i>
              <b>
               b
              </b>
             </i>
            </sup>
           </a>
          </span>
          <span class="reference-text">
           The leading hemisphere is the hemisphere facing the direction of the orbital motion; the trailing hemisphere faces the reverse direction.
          </span>
         </li>
         <li id="cite_note-45">
          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
           <b>
            <a href="#cite_ref-45">
             ^
            </a>
           </b>
          </span>
          <span class="reference-text">
           The dimensionless moment of inertia referred to is
           <span class="mwe-math-element">
            <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
             <math alttext="{\displaystyle I/(mr^{2})}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
              <semantics>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
                 <mi>
                  I
                 </mi>
                 <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                  <mo>
                   /
                  </mo>
                 </mrow>
                 <mo stretchy="false">
                  (
                 </mo>
                 <mi>
                  m
                 </mi>
                 <msup>
                  <mi>
                   r
                  </mi>
                  <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                   <mn>
                    2
                   </mn>
                  </mrow>
                 </msup>
                 <mo stretchy="false">
                  )
                 </mo>
                </mstyle>
               </mrow>
               <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
                {\displaystyle I/(mr^{2})}
               </annotation>
              </semantics>
             </math>
            </span>
            <img alt="{\displaystyle I/(mr^{2})}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e4f080de088a3314a70cbd82f30b520439f8eb9e" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:8.287ex; height:3.176ex;"/>
           </span>
           , where
           <var style="padding-right: 1px;">
            I
           </var>
           is the moment of inertia,
           <var style="padding-right: 1px;">
            m
           </var>
           the mass, and
           <var style="padding-right: 1px;">
            r
           </var>
           the maximal radius. It is 0.4 for a homogenous spherical body, but less than 0.4 if density increases with depth.
          </span>
         </li>
         <li id="cite_note-50">
          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
           <b>
            <a href="#cite_ref-50">
             ^
            </a>
           </b>
          </span>
          <span class="reference-text">
           In the case of icy satellites, palimpsests are defined as bright circular surface features, probably old impact craters
           <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greeley_2000_23-6">
            <a href="#cite_note-Greeley_2000-23">
             [17]
            </a>
           </sup>
          </span>
         </li>
         <li id="cite_note-51">
          <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
           <b>
            <a href="#cite_ref-51">
             ^
            </a>
           </b>
          </span>
          <span class="reference-text">
           To
           <i>
            embay
           </i>
           means to shut in, or shelter, as in a bay.
          </span>
         </li>
        </ol>
